In summary, the FBI broke with its standard practice on investigations, tilting the election to a candidate who then may have used that as a pretext to interfere with an investigation into him, and now could use it to stop the results of that investigation from becoming public.
But now some fear that the DOJ will use the "derogatory information" standard to withhold the most damning parts of the report from Congress and the public.
The president cannot be indicted, under DOJ guidelines, and there may not be evidence that he did anything wrong in a report filed by the special counsel.
The firing of Comey misfires, however, and the deputy attorney general ends up appointing a special counsel to look into the Russia stuff.
After winning the election, Trump fired Comey, over the Russia investigations, as he later admitted, using a letter from the deputy attorney general giving the stated reason that Comey had gone against the rule on derogatory information.
Some people make the case that the Comey letter was a major reason why Trump won the election, in which Clinton had been favored.
Because he had done that, he felt obligated to notify Congress when new evidence was found, which turned out to be inconsequential.
FBI Director James Comey went against that practice when he decided to speak out publicly about the reasoning behind their decisions on the Hillary Clinton email scandal.
The FBI's general practice is not to release derogatory information about people who it ends up deciding not to charge with a crime.

I don't want to compare any campaign to "Bohemian Rhapsody" because it would be too mean.
Kirsten Gillibrand is "BlacKkKlansman." A new offering from someone who's been around for a while and whose style and tone has shifted over the years.
Beto O'Rourke is "A Star Is Born." Fans love it, though critics poked some holes in the storyline. It's a classic narrative of an up-and-comer. Will voters buy it?
Elizabeth Warren is "The Favourite." Meaty and substantive, it ticks a lot of boxes in theory but might also be too much of a change.
("Vice" would have been the obvious joke here for Biden.)
Joe Biden is "Green Book." At one point, this would have been a clear frontrunner, since it's so made to order. But it's held back by some misfires.
Bernie Sanders is "Black Panther." It made a lot of money, which can't be discounted, but history says in the end voters won't go for it.
I know I'm a narrow target audience, but I would 100% read a piece comparing each of the Best Picture nominees to 2020 candidates.

@jessicashortall I was interviewing him. My grandma, who was in the middle stages of Alzheimer's, was nearby, in kind of a fog. He started getting weepy about his dear mother. Suddenly, grandma perks up, says "Oh she wasn't all that" and then settles back into the fog.
He died just a few months after turning 100. Here is the Stetson hat and some bolo ties that he always wore in retirement, since he didn't feel dressed without a tie of some sort. https://t.co/s3re3yBCYR
He was born on Sept. 13 and met my grandmother on the 13th, so whenever he made a business deal he always included a 13 somewhere in the offer.
He also witnessed the liberation of a concentration camp from across a hill (I don't know which one), but he wasn't close enough to realize how bad it was until after the war when the photos came out.
But he said he didn't want to be a "shirk," and ended up leaving the church. It's clear that he didn't really want to kill either, and he never was put in that situation. At one point, he carried a bazooka, but he never got close enough to a German tank to fire it.
As a member of the Church of the Brethren, one of the major pacifist denominations, he could have avoided service. His parents even brought him the forms to declare himself a conscientious objector.
One day one of the Germans comes up to him and says "Herr Beckwith, I speak English now." Then he rattled off every filthy curse word he'd heard a GI ever use, followed by "OK, let's go!" Everyone laughed.
He spoke a little German and knew how to type, so he ended up helping draw up maps and running errands for officers instead of being shot at. Part of his job was to talk with the three German civilians who helped.
A lifelong teetotaler, he asked for liquor and cigarette rations when he was in the military. He'd stuff them in his duffel bag and then sell them when they had a layover someplace like Morocco.
A 15-cent profit margin! Not bad. Grandpa was always a horse trader like that. His plans to be a doctor were sidetracked by World War II and he ended up becoming an ag teacher at the high school.
He would then take the gophers to his father, who paid him 10 cents for each one. And then he'd cut off the feet and take those to the county, which paid another 10 cents. Then he'd give Marjorie five cents.
My grandfather grew up in Iowa. Gophers were a problem on the farm, so his father showed him how to catch them. But he didn't like getting his hands dirty, so he got his sister, Marjorie, to set up the traps.

He relied on what we call "herd immunity" ‚Äî if most people were vaccinated, then the worst viruses couldn't get a foothold, and he'd never be exposed. But that meant the rest of us needed to do our part.
His immune system was compromised. Even a minor infection from the outside world could turn into a serious medical setback, perhaps even life-threatening.
My cousin had leukemia. As a teenager, I would visit him in the cancer ward at Fred Hutchinson in Seattle. All of us would wash our hands thoroughly before we went in. I was afraid to scratch my nose for fear I'd bring in a germ.
That's the science. Now let's talk on an emotional level.

Also, this is a super weak crutch in a debate. There are lots of good arguments to be made about these ideas, but trying to define them as out of bounds is cheap, doesn't work and leaves your defenses down.
Also, who are these expensive advisors? They should have flagged this for him the first time he did it and cut this off before it became a habit. Now he's done it three times.
I think this is just rookie candidate stuff, the kind of language you throw around with your friends that plays much differently once you become a candidate. But it highlights how much he has to learn if he wants to do this right.
Plus, all the things he's mentioned are within the bounds of current debate. America has had higher taxation rates before, lots of mainstream candidates are debating health care and everyone has opinions on spoiler candidates.
More importantly, READ THE ROOM. Voters who are disaffected from Trump often cite his attacks on political and democratic norms. Calling things "un-American" is on that same spectrum.
To be fair, candidates from both parties throw around "un-American," but it's usually reserved for the occasional reference. This is three times in a single week. That's overkill.

Finally, ask what they would be willing to do to achieve their goals. An ambitious climate change deal might only be possible through budget reconciliation or nuking the filibuster. Would they support that?
Again, if they don't specify, then assume they probably won't be going for whatever your pet topic is, especially if it's more politically complicated, costly or ambitious.
The next question is priorities. There's simply no way the next Democratic president will be able to achieve all of the goals that they'll be outlining. So which one comes first? Which do they spend their capital on?
Generally, the less detail they give about a proposal, the more you should assume they'll pursue a minimalist version of the policy, once the general election is underway.
The first question to ask is how they define these. One candidate might define Medicare for All as a Canadian-style single payer. Another might define it as a public option for 55+ Americans to buy into if they want.
They will probably even agree, in principle, to some big picture ideas, like Medicare for All. There may be a split among more pragmatic candidates and more idealistic ones on some issues.
Except for a few outliers, most of the candidates are going to broadly agree on the issues, especially vis a vis Trump: immigration, health care, climate change, etc.
The Democratic primary field will be crowded. If you're trying to understand it, keep a few things in mind:

Or you could have a narrative that stressed the role that chance plays, like that not-great movie "Sliding Doors," where Gwyneth Paltrow's life is greatly changed by missing a single train.
The ST:TNG episode "Tapestry" got at this notion by having Picard revisit a mistake in his youth, realizing that when he replayed the ending, his life was actually worse for not having made it.
Bandersnatch could have done this by making a clear tradeoff: You can have an ending where the videogame achieves greatness but at a terrible price, or an ending where he fails but lives through it.
‚ÄòRent Doesn‚Äôt Get Furloughed.‚Äô Federal Workers Are Missing Their First Paychecks As the Shutdown Drags On https://t.co/FnPGMuansn
The tradeoff, then, is the meaning. The point of the narrative is that you can't have it all and your choices show your values.
For example, there was a videogame once where at the end, you could either save your girlfriend or stop the poison from going into the city's water supply. You couldn't do both.
The solution, I think, is to make the different endings relate to each other.
In a multiple-choice narrative, then, the problem is that the different endings can make it feel pointless.
The ending is what gives the work meaning. If Ilsa and Rick run off together, everything that came before means something different.
I wrote about Choose Your Own Adventures a few years ago and have thought about the problem of the ending for a while.
